Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations

His name being absent from the current project report template, I am using this location to thank my 
supervisor Anand Natarajan for his time and teaching, within and beyond the scope of this project. 
1 Abstract 
Offshore wind turbine jacket structures have fatigue damage as a critical design limit state, which needs to be 
evaluated using aero-elastic simulations to satisfy an intended operational lifetime. 
This project used an existing HAWC2 model of the DTU 10MW reference turbine mounted on a jacket 
foundation. A post-treatment method was implemented in Python to compute fatigue damage for all tubular 
connections of the jacket model. 
Some simulation files were provided for all wind speeds to be considered, which allowed the determination 
of the fatigue life of all welded joints, using Palmgren-Miner’s rule. A variation of the directions distribution 
allowed to determine the most favourable orientation of the jacket as compared to the dominant wind. It has 
also been found that the use of S-N curves for the jacket in seawater without cathodic protection, although 
greatly reducing fatigue life as compared to the case with cathodic protection, does not significantly affect 
which nodes are the most critical. 
Some sensitivity cases were then simulated for two wind speeds, as follows, with in each case multiple 
simulations with yaw error and different seeds to increase the reliability of results: 
• 
Sensitivity to wind direction within direction bin, with three sets equally spaced by 10° being 
compared; 
• 
Sensitivity to wave spectrum, with Pierson-Moskowitz being compared to JONSWAP for the central 
directions set. 
The study of the variability of fatigue damage due to the yaw error and different seeds confirmed that multiple 
simulations are necessary to obtain reliable results. 
The direction sensitivity cases displayed a significant variability of fatigue results, showing that multiple wind 
directions within each direction bin should be simulated and involved in the fatigue life determination. Further 
studies would be necessary to determine how many would be sufficient. 
The spectrum sensitivity cases showed that the selection of the wave spectrum has a significant influence on 
the evaluation of fatigue life, and that the wave spectrum should therefore be carefully selected based on the 
metocean data. 
In the process, it was also found that Rainflow counting functions in Python and in Matlab give different cycle 
counts and ranges, resulting in a significantly different fatigue damage. Further studies would be necessary to 
determine if one of them is erroneous.

3 Introduction 
According to the definition of DNVGL Ref. [b], “Jackets substructure/foundations are typically three- or four-
legged triangulated structures all made of circular steel tubes. On top of the jacket structure is installed a 
transition piece, typically a plated structure, which is designed with a large centre steel tube for connection 
with the tower. The jacket is typically anchored into the seabed by piles installed at each jacket leg.”.  Figure 
1 shows how it compares with other types of support structures. 
 
Figure 1 - Various concepts of offshore wind turbine support structures (Ref. [f]) 
 
Figure 2 – Typical jacket arrangement (Ref. [f]) 
While monopiles, which are the simplest support structures, are still by far the most common type (see Figure 
3), it is generally considered that they become uneconomical beyond a water depth of around 45 𝑚𝑚. Jackets, 
on the other hand, while more complex in their arrangement and construction, make more efficient use of the 
==PAGE:10==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
9 
steel (due to the material being at a greater distance from the central axis of the turbine, requiring less weight 
for an equivalent strength and stiffness), and are therefore applicable up to a depth of around 70 𝑚𝑚 with the 
current state of the art and economical parameters, according to the course Ref. [f]. 
With new windfarms moving to greater water depths where jackets are competitive with monopiles, or even 
beyond that level, jackets have become in 2018 the second most common type of substructures, overtaking 
GBSs and representing 24.5 % of new installations for that year. 
 
Figure 3 - Number of grid-connected offshore wind turbines at the end of 2018 for each type of support structures (Ref. [g]) 
A recent example is shown on Figure 4: Beatrice offshore wind farm, with 84 × 7 𝑀𝑀𝑀𝑀 Siemens Gamesa 
turbines installed between 2018 and 2019, on jackets in water depths ranging from 35 𝑚𝑚 to 65 𝑚𝑚. 
==PAGE:11==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
10 
 
Figure 4 – Beatrice offshore wind farm, offshore Scotland 
Just like any other offshore wind turbine support structure, jackets are subject to highly varying loads due to 
wind and waves, making their design very complex. 
The rotor and nacelle are generally designed to a standard class from IEC Ref. [c], defining a reference wind 
speed and turbulence intensity, making the same design suitable for a great number of sites (see Table 1).  
 
Table 1 - Basic parameters for wind turbine classes (IEC 61400-1, Ref. {c]) 
The support structure, on the other hand, having a greater number of design input parameters, is always 
designed for the specific site. Requirements for the design of support structures for wind turbines, both 
onshore and fixed offshore, are covered in a DNVGL standard (ST-0126 Ref. [b]). This covers in particular – for 
global design verifications, local details and geotechnical design – requirements and criteria to be verified for 
ULS (maximum load-carrying resistance), FLS (long-term resistance to cyclic loads), ALS (resistance to 
accidental loads, or post-accident integrity) and SLS (criteria for normal use). 
In the design of jacket structures, it is often found that achieving a 25-year fatigue life (usually specified by the 
project, rather than the minimum 20 years required by Ref. [b]) of the tubular connections for the defined FLS 
criteria is one of the most critical parts of the design process. It is therefore desirable that a structural engineer 
such as the author should follow a reliable process to evaluate fatigue life, in order to produce a suitable and 
economical design. Thus it is of great interest to know how variations of environmental conditions affect the 
evaluation of fatigue life. 
==PAGE:12==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations

4 HAWC2 simulations 
4.1 Jacket model 
A HAWC2 four-legged / 50m jacket model with the DTU 10MW reference turbine (Ref. [i]) was provided for 
the study, similar (but not identical) to the one used for the research report Ref. [j].  
In order to understand the geometry, a structural model was re-created with NSO/FASTRUDL, giving the 
ISYMOST visualization shown in Figure 5. Figure 6 can be used to identify the location of tubular connections 
analyzed in this study. 
 
 
Figure 5 – Visualization of the structural model with ISYMOST

Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report

13 
4.2 DLCs 
IEC Ref. [d] defines all DLCs to be taken into account in a fixed offshore wind turbine design. For fatigue, the 
main one is DLC 1.2 (normal power production), therefore the present study will concentrate on this one. 
The whole range of wind speeds between cut-in and cut-out shall be covered, and the effect of atmospheric 
turbulence has to be taken into account. No current is needed, and IEC allows the use of a single value of 
significant wave height for each wind speed bin, to be selected based on the long-term joint probability 
distribution of metocean parameters. 
4.3 Environmental conditions 
Environmental conditions from INNWIND Ref. [j] were used, as shown in Table 2. 
The base case was considered as a JONSWAP wave spectrum with a peak factor of 3.3. Some simulations were 
also done with a Pierson-Moskowitz spectrum using the same 𝐻𝐻𝑠𝑠 and 𝑇𝑇𝑝𝑝. 
This was combined with three wind directions distributions given in Table 3, with distribution 1 being as shown 
in Figure 7, and distributions 2 and 3 being rotations by 30° and 60° of the former. Note that any further 
rotation by a multiple of 30° would be equivalent to one of the three distributions selected, since the jacket 
has four identical faces. 
The wind speeds frequency distribution was considered as identical for all directions, therefore the number of 
hours and directions distributions were included in the Python code as independent 1D arrays. However, with 
a bit more complexity these could be replaced by 2D arrays giving joint probability distributions (scatter 
diagram) if necessary. 
In all cases, the waves were considered as aligned with the wind in order to maximize the loads on the jacket. 
 
Table 2 – Environmental conditions for the wind and wave parameters, and their expected annual frequency

5 Post-treatment of simulation results 
5.1 Geometry database 
Some text files were provided (Attachments 1 and 2), giving: 
• 
the nodes coordinates, in a Bladed-type reference system (Z is vertical upwards). The coordinates 
were not transformed to the HAWC2 reference system, since only the relative position of nodes is of 
importance; 
• 
the members connectivity and cross section characteristics. Note that all members have constant 
characteristics over their length, and that no marine growth is taken into account. 
Three Excel databases (provided in Attachments 4 to 6) were created based on the files described above, as 
well as on the Orientation.dat file of the HAWC2 model which gives the Euler parameters for all members 
(Attachment 3), one for each type of connections: Y-joints (Figure 8), X-joints (Figure 9) and K-joints (Figure 
10). 
In the databases, the following data was defined by hand for each tubular connection: 
• 
The node number of the point of connection. All nodes on the legs are repeated twice: indeed, braces 
defining (with the chord) connection planes at more than 10° from each other have to be treated as 
independent connections, therefore these node have one connection per jacket panel; 
• 
The number of the braces and chords members; 
• 
For each brace, the number of the channel where the time history of force in X-direction is stored in 
the results file (other components of forces and moments are always in the subsequent channels). 
The rest of the databases was filled automatically with the VLOOKUP function over the data of the text files 
described above, with the following: 
• 
Coordinates of all nodes involved in the braces and chords; 
• 
Euler parameters of the braces; 
• 
OD and WT of the braces and chords. 
One parameter of importance for the determination of SCFs was not included in the databases, as it is easier 
to compute in the Python post-treatment code (based on the coordinates of the brace and chord nodes): the 
angle Θ between the brace and the chord. 
Please note that the following modifications were brought to the thicknesses in the database in order to 
display acceptable fatigue results: 
• 
Braces 29 to 44: 30 𝑚𝑚𝑚𝑚 instead of 20 𝑚𝑚𝑚𝑚; 
• 
Braces 45 to 60: 32 𝑚𝑚𝑚𝑚 instead of 20.581 𝑚𝑚𝑚𝑚; 
• 
Braces 77 to 92: 90 𝑚𝑚𝑚𝑚 instead of 80 𝑚𝑚𝑚𝑚; 
• 
Legs 9 to 12: 48 𝑚𝑚𝑚𝑚 instead of 44.846 𝑚𝑚𝑚𝑚. 
This should be seen as a pre-design, and it would be necessary to re-run the HAWC2 simulations to take into 
the influence of the modified thicknesses (leg members in particular) on the resulting load histories. This is 
not done here as the goal is not to obtain a satisfactory design, but to compare the results for different 
situations.

5.2 Loads extraction 
The results files from HAWC2 contain time histories of three force components and three moment at each 
node of each member, in a local reference system which is based on the Euler parameters given in the 
assembly part of the HAWC2 model, with just enough rotations to bring each member to its place. However, 
for the stress computation defined in DNVGL Ref. [a], only three components are considered as playing a 
significant role, and they have to be expressed in a structural reference system as follows: 
• 
Axial force; 
• 
In-plane bending moment (i.e. in the plane of the tubular connection of interest); 
• 
Out-of-plane bending moment (i.e. perpendicular to the previous component). 
Therefore the first action once the time histories are loaded is to recombine the raw data in local reference 
into the three time series in structural reference, using Euler parameters contained in the database for each 
brace of each connection. 
5.3 SCFs 
SCFs for all joints in the database are computed in part of the post-treatment Python code shown in 
Attachment 16, using the formulae given in DNVGL Ref. [a] Appendix B, which are based on the work of 
Efthymiou in Ref. [l] involving over 150 FEMs of tubular connections from which empirical equations were 
deduced. 
The first step is to define the dimensionless geometrical parameters as given in Figure 11. 
==PAGE:20==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
19 
 
Figure 11 – Definition of geometrical parameters 
 
 
==PAGE:21==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
20 
The determination of one of the parameters is problematic: 𝛼𝛼=
2𝐿𝐿
𝐷𝐷 where 𝐿𝐿 is the effective chord length and 
𝐷𝐷 the chord outer diameter. The effective chord length is easy to determine for a testing configuration or an 
FEM such as used in the work of Efthymiou (distance between the chord supports), but it is more difficult to 
select in the case of a complex structure with several structural nodes. 
DNVGL Ref. [a] offers no guideline to this aim, but API Ref. [k], which uses the same definition of SCFs, discusses 
this point in more detail. For jacket joints with a thick can substituted to part of the chord (wind turbine jackets 
seldom use these as cost effectiveness is more important than for oil platforms), the length is simply that of 
the reinforced portion. Where there is no such thickness transition, a general expression to relate the 
connection of interest to a local model is to determine the effective length based on the in-plane bending 
moment 𝑀𝑀 and the transverse load 𝑃𝑃 applied to the chord by the brace: 𝐿𝐿=
4𝑀𝑀
𝑃𝑃. However this has two major 
drawbacks: 
• 
The effective length, and thus the SCFs, have to be recomputed at every time step based on the 
current load pattern; 
• 
The chord has two members, one on each side of the node, which have different values of in-plane 
bending moment. 
Thus API recommends a simplified approach, which is used in this report: assume a standard 𝛼𝛼= 12 and use 
the chord-end fixity parameter 𝐶𝐶= 0.5 for all connections. 
A condition for the SCF formulae included in the standard to be valid for a specific connection is that its 
parameters should fall within the following ranges: 
 
The verification of these ranges shows that for our jacket model:  
• 
The condition for 𝛾𝛾 is not met, due to the chord being too thick, for: half of the X-joints, all of the K-
joints and Y-joints; 
• 
The condition for 𝜏𝜏 is not met, due to the brace being thicker than the chord, for: a third of the K-joints 
and all of the Y-joints. 
In such a case, DNVGL gives no specific guideline, therefore the general procedure of determining the SCFs by 
FEA applies. This was not a possibility for this study, therefore a simplified approach of using the standard 
formulae while limiting the dimensionless parameters to the ranges boundaries was adopted, as was 
apparently the case for the INNWIND study Ref. [j]. For comparison, API recommends to use the worst case 
between this simplified approach and the standard equations without limiting the parameters to the 
boundaries. 
It is however worth noting that parameter 𝜏𝜏 exceeding a value of 1.0 is not just a problem for calculations, as 
it is normally not allowed in fabrication to have a brace thicker than its chord due to potential problems of 
weldability. 
==PAGE:22==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
21 
A last consideration is that for X-joints and K-joints, the equations to be used for the determination of SCFs 
depend on the load pattern, i.e. whether a brace load in the chord transverse direction is in balance with a 
load in another brace or with shear in the chord. DNVGL states that “a conservative classification may be used 
keeping in mind that: SCFX > SCFY > SCFK”, meaning in practice that the usual method is: 
• 
To take the SCFs for the balanced (“X”) case for X-joints; 
• 
To take the SCFs for loads on one brace only (“Y”) for K-joints. 
This is the method that was selected in order to be comparable to other studies. However the Python code 
does include parameters representing the proportion of X/Y load pattern for X-joints and Y/K load pattern for 
K-joints. A part has been commented out but is still included in Attachment 16, where these parameters were 
defined as arrays with a value in the [0;1] range for each time step based on the loads in the current and 
opposite braces. These parameters are currently set to a single fixed value of 0 or 1 depending on the desired 
type of SCFs.

chord points around the circumference of the weld, as shown in Figure 13  (Figure 12 shows the location of 
crown and saddle points): 
• 
Crown points 1 and 5; 
• 
Saddle points 3 and 7; 
• 
Intermediate points 2, 4, 6 and 8.

5.5 Damage and fatigue life computation 
Once the stress history of each of the 16 points to be checked for each tubular weld is obtained, we want to 
compute the resulting fatigue damage for the 10-minute simulation. For the cases with cathodic protection, 
this is done with the following function, which takes the thickness as an argument along with the stress signal: 
def damage_seaCP(sig,WT): 
    rang, mean = rainflow_astm(sig) 
    sigt=np.maximum(mean+rang/2,0) 
    abssigc=np.maximum(rang/2-mean,0) 
    rang=rang*(sigt+0.8*abssigc)/(sigt+abssigc) 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    Damage=np.sum(0.5/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
 
The steps are as follows: 
• 
the stress history is decomposed into half-cycles with the Rainflow counting function “rainflow_astm” 
(in this function, full cycles are output as a repetition of two half-cycles with the same characteristics); 
• 
the stress range is then corrected to take the mean stress influence into account with a reduction 
factor as per equation (2.5.2) of DNVGL Ref. [a]; 
• 
for each half-cycle, the number of cycles to failure for the effective stress range is computed using the 
S-N curve. Note that for this S-N curve which is linear by parts over two domains, computing the values 
for the two slopes and taking the maximum between the two is equivalent to taking the steeper slope 
on the left side of the transition and the other slope to the right (as shown in Figure 14). Besides, 
taking a “np.maximum” of two arrays is computationally far more efficient than looping through the 
half-cycles to test which slope should be selected; 
• 
the total damage for the signal, computed using the Palmgren-Miner linear damage hypothesis, is the 
sum of individual damages for all half-cycles (i.e. 0.5 over the number of cycles to failure). 
The S-N curves are defined as follows, including a thickness effect: 
 
Where: 
m = negative inverse slope of the S - N curve 
 = intercept of log N axis 
tref = reference thickness equal to 16mm for tubular joints 
t = thickness of the part considered 
k = thickness exponent on fatigue strength 
 
==PAGE:25==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
24 
The values used for the parameters are the ones given by DNVGL for tubular joints (Table 4). Note the all 
joints were analyzed with the curves in seawater, either with (all cases) or without (results files provided 
for all wind speeds only) cathodic protection. 
 
 
Table 4 – S-N curves for tubular joints 
 
 
 
Figure 14 – S-N curve for tubular joints 
All fatigue values obtained at a same point for 10-minute simulations with different wind speeds and directions 
are combined using the appropriate directions distribution from Table 3 and the expected annual duration in 
wind speed bins from Table 2, which gives the expected annual damage at this point. The inverse of this is 
taken with a safety factor of 3 to obtain the fatigue life (for joints above water, a lower factor of 2 is allowed 
but has not been considered here for simplicity). 
Note that fatigue damage occurring during stages of transportation, installation, pre-operation and shut-down 
have been ignored in this comparative study (in particular the number of hours used do not sum up to a full 
year and the remaining hours have been ignored), and that a real design needs to take those into account. 
Continuation of slope for 
high stress ranges 
Continuation of slope for low stress ranges 
Max value for the two slopes gives 
correct value for both high and low 
stress ranges 
Transition level 
==PAGE:26==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
25 
It should also be noted that a Matlab code, included in Attachment 24, was used to cross-check the damage 
computation by evaluating the damage at one point of one joint resulting from one simulation. Although it 
was verified that the signal before Rainflow counting was identical in Python and Matlab, and that there should 
be no mistake in the simple use of the S-N curve to compute the cumulative damage, the Matlab code gave 
around 30% less damage than the Python code. The difference therefore seems to come purely from the 
Rainflow counting functions, which output a different number of cycles with different ranges. 
Several slower implementations of the Rainflow function were tested and are still present in the Python code: 
these gave results which were close to the original Python code. It would be interesting to study further the 
difference between the Python and Matlab Rainflow counting functions. 
5.6 Sensitivity 
For the sensitivity cases, the post-treatment was the same except that, since only two wind speeds were used, 
it stopped at the computation of the annual damage for the number of hours in those wind speed bins. The 
six simulations available for each direction (with different yaw errors and seeds) are combined with an equal 
weight, and the standard deviation of damage across those six cases is computed. 
Direction 1 with JONSWAP spectrum is considered as the base case, and all other cases (2 other directions 
with JONSWAP spectrum, PM spectrum for direction 1) are compared to it. 
The Python code for post-treatment of the base case is provided in Attachment 17. 
==PAGE:27==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
26 
6 Results and conclusion 
6.1 Fatigue life 
The estimated fatigue life of all connections, for the results file provided for all wind speeds, as described in Section 
4.3 and listed in Attachment 13, using the assumptions described in this report, are given in the following tables, with 
the most critical nodes being highlighted in red. 
Table 5 to Table 7 show the results with cathodic protection and their dependency upon the distribution of wind 
directions. Distribution 1 having always slightly worse results than distributions 2 and 3, it can therefore be concluded 
that it is preferable to have the dominant wind direction along a diagonal of the jacket, as in Figure 15. 
 
Figure 15 – Most favourable jacket position with regards to dominant winds 
Then Table 8 to Table 16 compare the results for all nodes and all directions distributions, with and without cathodic 
protection. Although fatigue life is as expected dramatically reduced in the absence of cathodic protection, the results 
show that the most critical nodes remain the same for the K-joints and Y-joints, and are only slightly reordered for the 
X-joints. 
Figure 16 to Figure 24 show the distribution of annual damage over wind speed bins for all directions distributions, 
and for the most critical connection of each type. These show that 17 𝑚𝑚/𝑠𝑠 and 19 𝑚𝑚/𝑠𝑠 wind speed bins often have 
some of the highest contribution to fatigue damage of the connections, which is why they were selected for the 
sensitivity analyses. 
The same figures for all connections, with or without cathodic protection, are also given in Attachments 18 to 22.

Figure 24 – Directions distribution 3 with cathodic protection – Annual damage vs wind speed at most critical Y-joint 
6.2 Sensitivity 
Table 17 to Table 32 give the results of the sensitivity study, with in each case the percentage of variation from the 
base case. 
The high coefficients of variation show that there is a high variability of fatigue damage depending on the yaw error 
and on the seeds used, and it is therefore critical to use a sufficient number of simulations to draw significant 
conclusions. 
The direction sensitivity cases show that annual fatigue damage is greatly affected by the directions chosen, with up 
to -65.2/+28.7% variation from the base case at 17 𝑚𝑚/𝑠𝑠, and -77.6/+144.8% at 19 𝑚𝑚/𝑠𝑠. This demonstrates that 12 
wind directions is an insufficient number, and that several directions per direction bin should be used. Further studies 
would be necessary to determine how many would be enough. 
The use of the Pierson-Moskowitz spectrum instead of the JONSWAP spectrum gives a limited annual damage variation 
of maximum -15.7/+20.5% for any connection at 17 𝑚𝑚/𝑠𝑠, but more significant values of up to -80.2/+21.5% at 19 𝑚𝑚/𝑠𝑠. 
Moreover the variation can be positive or negative depending on the connection. The wave spectrum should therefore 
be carefully selected based on the metocean data.

Attachment 7 – Y-connections SCFs – Without limitations to parameters boundaries 
These tables were created in Excel as part of a verification of the SCFs computed in Python. The braces angles (in radians) are copied from the Python post-treatment results 
(the values obtained are consistent), while the other geometrical parameters are computed in Excel directly from the connections database. In the tables below, the first 
column of each SCF is the value from the Python code, while the “Control” column to its right is the value computed in Excel. The name of the first column gives the identity 
of the SCF, for instance YSCF_C_AC is for: Y-joint SCF, chord side, axial force at crown.

values as explained in the corpus of the report. The same modification was then implemented in the Python code. This being a basic modification with no possible error, the

Attachment 10 – X-connections SCFs – With limitations to parameters boundaries  
The following tables contain the same values as the Python-computed SCFs in Attachment 8, but the control values computed in Excel are now with modified parameter 
values as explained in the corpus of the report. The same modification was then implemented in the Python code. This being a basic modification with no possible error, the 
new values in Python are not printed out again since Attachment 8 already shows that the two computation methods match with each other.

97 
Attachment 11 – K-connections SCFs – Without limitations to parameters boundaries 
These tables were created in Excel as part of a verification of the SCFs computed in Python. The braces angles (in radians) are copied from the Python post-treatment results 
(the values obtained are consistent), while the other geometrical parameters are computed in Excel directly from the connections database. In the tables below, the first 
column of each SCF is the value from the Python code, while the “Control” column to its right is the value computed in Excel. The name of the first column gives the identity 
of the SCF, for instance KY1SCF_C_AC is for: K-joint with Y load pattern SCF at brace 1, chord side, axial force at crown.

Attachment 12 – K-connections SCFs – With limitations to parameters boundaries 
The following tables contain the same values as the Python-computed SCFs in Attachment 11, but the control values computed in Excel are now with modified parameter 
values as explained in the corpus of the report. The same modification was then implemented in the Python code. This being a basic modification with no possible error, the 
new values in Python are not printed out again since Attachment 11 already shows that the two computation methods match with each other.

# Function to read the output of a simulation 
def read_sig(fname): 
    res = windIO.LoadResults(os.path.dirname(fname), os.path.basename(fname), 
                         readdata=True)    
    return res.sig

# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP(sig,WT): 
    ampl, mean = rainflow_astm(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(11.764-3*np.log10(ampl*(WT/32)**0.25)),10**(15.606-5*np.log10(ampl*(WT/32)**0.25))) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(0.5/N_failure) 
    return Damage

# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP_fatpack(sig,WT): 
    rang=fatpack.find_rainflow_ranges(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(1/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP(sig,WT): 
    rang, mean = rainflow_astm(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    sigt=np.maximum(mean+rang/2,0) 
    abssigc=np.maximum(rang/2-mean,0) 
    rang=rang*(sigt+0.8*abssigc)/(sigt+abssigc) 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(0.5/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater without cathodic protection 
def damage_sea_noCP(sig,WT): 
    rang, mean = rainflow_astm(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    sigt=np.maximum(mean+rang/2,0) 
    abssigc=np.maximum(rang/2-mean,0) 
    rang=rang*(sigt+0.8*abssigc)/(sigt+abssigc) 
    N_failure=10**(12.03-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(0.5/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP_range_only(sig,WT): 
    rang, mean = rainflow_astm(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(0.5/N_failure) 
    return Damage

def damage_seaCP_save3(sig,WT):

# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP_save(sig,WT): 
    cycles, ampl_edges, mean_edges = cycle_matrix2(sig, 50, 1) 
    cycles=cycles.reshape(50,) 
    #Central value of bins 
    bin_ampl=(ampl_edges[0:50]+ampl_edges[1:])/2 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(bin_ampl*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(bin_ampl*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(cycles/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
 
# Definition of a function to compute the 1Hz damage equivalent load of the selected 600s channel for the specified m exponent 
and number of bins 
def DEL_sig(sig,channelnum,mcurve,no_bins): 
# calculate the equivalent load manually for a random signal 
    eql = eq_load(sig[:,channelnum-1], no_bins=no_bins, m=mcurve, neq=600) 
    return eql[0][0] 
 
 
 
# Definition of a function to compute the 1Hz damage equivalent load of the selected 600s channel for the specified m exponent 
and number of bins 
def DEL(series,mcurve,no_bins): 
# calculate the equivalent load manually for a random signal 
    eql = eq_load(series, no_bins=no_bins, m=mcurve, neq=600) 
    return eql[0][0] 
 
 
 
# Definition of a function to compute the mean value of the selected channel 
def meanvalue(sig,channelnum): 
    return np.mean(sig[:,channelnum-1]) 
 
 
# Function to return the brace loads for a joint with 1 brace 
def Ybrace_loads(sig,Yclass): 
    Fz=sig[:,Yclass[5]+1] 
    Mx=sig[:,Yclass[5]+2] 
    My=sig[:,Yclass[5]+3] 
# Brace A Properties 
    node1_A = np.array([Yclass[13] ,  Yclass[14] ,  Yclass[15]]) 
    node2_A = np.array([Yclass[17] ,  Yclass[18] ,  Yclass[19]]) 
    eulpar_A = np.array([Yclass[6],   Yclass[7] ,  Yclass[8] , Yclass[9]]) 
    LENGTH_A =  np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
 
# Chord Properties 
    NODE1 = np.array([Yclass[48] ,  Yclass[49] ,  Yclass[50]]) 
    NODE2 = np.array([Yclass[52] ,  Yclass[53] ,  Yclass[54]]) 
    LENGTH =  np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
     
    Bladed2Hawc2 = np.array([[0, 1, 0], [1, 0, 0], [0, 0, -1]]) 
    node1_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node1_A.conj().T 
    node2_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node2_A.conj().T 
    NODE1 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE1.conj().T 
    NODE2 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE2.conj().T 
 
# Member directions 
    Vect_A = (node2_A - node1_A)/np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
    Vect_chord = (NODE2 - NODE1)/np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
 
# Side normal 
    Side_direct = 1/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord))*np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord) 
 
# Global to Local transformation matrix 
 
    Glob2Loca_A=np.zeros((3,3)) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][0] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 + (eulpar_A[1])**2 - (eulpar_A[2])**2 - (eulpar_A[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][1] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[2]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][2] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[3]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][0] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[2]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][1] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 - (eulpar_A[1])**2 + (eulpar_A[2])**2 - (eulpar_A[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][2] = 2*((eulpar_A[2]) * (eulpar_A[3]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[1])) 
==PAGE:118==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
117 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][0] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[3]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][1] = 2*((eulpar_A[2]) * (eulpar_A[3]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][2] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 - (eulpar_A[1])**2 - (eulpar_A[2])**2 + (eulpar_A[3])**2 
 
# Global to Structural transformation matrix for brace A 
    axis3 = -Vect_A                                          
    axis1 = Side_direct                                                  
    axis2 = np.cross(axis1, axis3)/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(axis1, axis3))                   
    Glob2Struc_A = np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).conj() 
#Glob2Struc_A = (np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).T).conj().T 
 
    Loca2Struc_A=Glob2Struc_A@np.linalg.pinv(Glob2Loca_A) 
     
    Fa=Loca2Struc_A[2][2]*Fz 
    Mip=Loca2Struc_A[0][0]*Mx+Loca2Struc_A[0][1]*My 
    Mop=Loca2Struc_A[1][0]*Mx+Loca2Struc_A[1][1]*My 
     
    return Fa, Mip, Mop 
 
 
 
 
 
 
# Function to return the brace loads for a joint with two braces 
def XKbrace_loads(sig,XKclass): 
    FzA=sig[:,XKclass[5]+1] 
    MxA=sig[:,XKclass[5]+2] 
    MyA=sig[:,XKclass[5]+3] 
     
    FzB=sig[:,XKclass[25]+1] 
    MxB=sig[:,XKclass[25]+2] 
    MyB=sig[:,XKclass[25]+3] 
# Brace A Properties 
    node1_A = np.array([XKclass[13] ,  XKclass[14] ,  XKclass[15]]) 
    node2_A = np.array([XKclass[17] ,  XKclass[18] ,  XKclass[19]]) 
    eulpar_A = np.array([XKclass[6],   XKclass[7] ,  XKclass[8] , XKclass[9]]) 
    LENGTH_A =  np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
     
# Brace B Properties 
    node1_B = np.array([XKclass[33] ,  XKclass[34] ,  XKclass[35]]) 
    node2_B = np.array([XKclass[37] ,  XKclass[38] ,  XKclass[39]]) 
    eulpar_B = np.array([XKclass[26],   XKclass[27] ,  XKclass[28] , XKclass[29]]) 
    LENGTH_B =  np.linalg.norm(node2_B - node1_B) 
 
# Chord Properties 
    NODE1 = np.array([XKclass[48] ,  XKclass[49] ,  XKclass[50]]) 
    NODE2 = np.array([XKclass[52] ,  XKclass[53] ,  XKclass[54]]) 
    LENGTH =  np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
     
    Bladed2Hawc2 = np.array([[0, 1, 0], [1, 0, 0], [0, 0, -1]]) 
    node1_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node1_A.conj().T 
    node2_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node2_A.conj().T 
    node1_B = Bladed2Hawc2@node1_B.conj().T 
    node2_B = Bladed2Hawc2@node2_B.conj().T 
    NODE1 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE1.conj().T 
    NODE2 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE2.conj().T 
 
# Member directions 
    Vect_A = (node2_A - node1_A)/np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
    Vect_B = (node2_B - node1_B)/np.linalg.norm(node2_B - node1_B) 
    Vect_chord = (NODE2 - NODE1)/np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
 
# Side normal 
    Side_directA = 1/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord))*np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord) 
    Side_directB = 1/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(Vect_B, Vect_chord))*np.cross(Vect_B, Vect_chord) 
 
# Global to Local transformation matrix 
 
    Glob2Loca_A=np.zeros((3,3)) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][0] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 + (eulpar_A[1])**2 - (eulpar_A[2])**2 - (eulpar_A[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][1] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[2]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][2] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[3]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][0] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[2]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][1] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 - (eulpar_A[1])**2 + (eulpar_A[2])**2 - (eulpar_A[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][2] = 2*((eulpar_A[2]) * (eulpar_A[3]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][0] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[3]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][1] = 2*((eulpar_A[2]) * (eulpar_A[3]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][2] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 - (eulpar_A[1])**2 - (eulpar_A[2])**2 + (eulpar_A[3])**2 
     
    Glob2Loca_B=np.zeros((3,3)) 
    Glob2Loca_B[0][0] = (eulpar_B[0])**2 + (eulpar_B[1])**2 - (eulpar_B[2])**2 - (eulpar_B[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_B[0][1] = 2*((eulpar_B[1]) * (eulpar_B[2]) + (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[0][2] = 2*((eulpar_B[1]) * (eulpar_B[3]) - (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[1][0] = 2*((eulpar_B[1]) * (eulpar_B[2]) - (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[1][1] = (eulpar_B[0])**2 - (eulpar_B[1])**2 + (eulpar_B[2])**2 - (eulpar_B[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_B[1][2] = 2*((eulpar_B[2]) * (eulpar_B[3]) + (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[2][0] = 2*((eulpar_B[1]) * (eulpar_B[3]) + (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[2][1] = 2*((eulpar_B[2]) * (eulpar_B[3]) - (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[2][2] = (eulpar_B[0])**2 - (eulpar_B[1])**2 - (eulpar_B[2])**2 + (eulpar_B[3])**2 
 
# Global to Structural transformation matrix 
    axis3A = -Vect_A                                          
    axis1A = Side_directA                                                  
    axis2A = np.cross(axis1A, axis3A)/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(axis1A, axis3A))                   
    Glob2Struc_A = np.array([axis1A,axis2A,axis3A]).conj() 
==PAGE:119==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
118 
     
    axis3B = -Vect_B                                          
    axis1B = Side_directB                                                  
    axis2B = np.cross(axis1B, axis3B)/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(axis1B, axis3B))                   
    Glob2Struc_B = np.array([axis1B,axis2B,axis3B]).conj() 
     
#Glob2Struc_A = (np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).T).conj().T 
 
    Loca2Struc_A=Glob2Struc_A@np.linalg.pinv(Glob2Loca_A) 
    Loca2Struc_B=Glob2Struc_B@np.linalg.pinv(Glob2Loca_B) 
     
    FaA=Loca2Struc_A[2][2]*FzA 
    MipA=Loca2Struc_A[0][0]*MxA+Loca2Struc_A[0][1]*MyA 
    MopA=Loca2Struc_A[1][0]*MxA+Loca2Struc_A[1][1]*MyA 
     
    FaB=Loca2Struc_B[2][2]*FzB 
    MipB=Loca2Struc_B[0][0]*MxB+Loca2Struc_B[0][1]*MyB 
    MopB=Loca2Struc_B[1][0]*MxB+Loca2Struc_B[1][1]*MyB 
     
    return FaA, MipA, MopA, FaB, MipB, MopB 
 
 
 
 
 
# Function to return the node 10 brace 34 loads 
#def brace_loads(joint,brace,sig): 
def brace34_loads(sig): 
    Fz=sig[:,561] 
    Mx=sig[:,562] 
    My=sig[:,563] 
# Brace A Properties 
    node1_A = np.array([11.326500000000 ,  0.000000000000 ,  -39.354600000000]) 
    node2_A = np.array([10.653000000000 ,  -10.653000000000 ,  -30.209200000000]) 
    eulpar_A = np.array([-0.27974,   0.63527 ,  0.64942 , -0.31053]) 
    LENGTH_A =  np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
 
# Leg Properties 
    NODE1 = np.array([12.000000000000 ,  -12.000000000000 ,  -48.500000000000]) 
    NODE2 = np.array([9.631990000000 ,  -9.631990000000 ,  -16.344900000000]) 
    LENGTH =  np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
     
    Bladed2Hawc2 = np.array([[0, 1, 0], [1, 0, 0], [0, 0, -1]]) 
    node1_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node1_A.conj().T 
    node2_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node2_A.conj().T 
    NODE1 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE1.conj().T 
    NODE2 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE2.conj().T 
 
# Member directions 
    Vect_A = (node2_A - node1_A)/np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
    Vect_chord = (NODE2 - NODE1)/np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
        
#Connection angle 
    theta=min(np.arccos(np.vdot(Vect_A,Vect_chord)),np.pi-np.arccos(np.vdot(Vect_A,Vect_chord))) 
 
# Side normal 
    Side_direct = 1/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord))*np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord) 
 
# Global to Local transformation matrix 
    e0 = eulpar_A[0] 
    e1 = eulpar_A[1] 
    e2 = eulpar_A[2] 
    e3 = eulpar_A[3] 
 
    Glob2Loca_A=np.zeros((3,3)) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][0] = e0**2 + e1**2 - e2**2 - e3**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][1] = 2*(e1 * e2 + e0 * e3) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][2] = 2*(e1 * e3 - e0 * e2) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][0] = 2*(e1 * e2 - e0 * e3) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][1] = e0**2 - e1**2 + e2**2 - e3**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][2] = 2*(e2 * e3 + e0 * e1) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][0] = 2*(e1 * e3 + e0 * e2) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][1] = 2*(e2 * e3 - e0 * e1) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][2] = e0**2 - e1**2 - e2**2 + e3**2 
 
# Global to Structural transformation matrix for brace A 
    axis3 = -Vect_A                                          
    axis1 = Side_direct                                                  
    axis2 = np.cross(axis1, axis3)/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(axis1, axis3))                   
    Glob2Struc_A = np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).conj() 
#Glob2Struc_A = (np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).T).conj().T 
 
    Loca2Struc_A=Glob2Struc_A@np.linalg.pinv(Glob2Loca_A) 
     
    Fa=Loca2Struc_A[2][2]*Fz 
    Mip=Loca2Struc_A[0][0]*Mx+Loca2Struc_A[0][1]*My 
    Mop=Loca2Struc_A[1][0]*Mx+Loca2Struc_A[1][1]*My 
     
    return Fa, Mip, Mop, theta

Kbeta1**2)**0.5*np.sin(2*Ktheta1)**2 
KY1SCF_C_MIP=1.45*Kbeta1*Ktau1**0.85*Kgamma**(1-0.68*Kbeta1)*np.sin(Ktheta1)**0.7 
KY1SCF_C_MOP=(Kgamma*Ktau1*Kbeta1*(1.7-1.05*Kbeta1**3)*np.sin(Ktheta1)**1.6)*(1-0.08*(Kbeta2*Kgamma)**0.5*np.exp(1)**(-
0.8*(1+Kzeta*np.sin(Ktheta1)/Kbeta1)))

KY1SCF_B_AS=1.3+Kgamma*Ktau1**0.52*Kalpha**0.1*(0.187-1.25*Kbeta1**1.1*(Kbeta1-0.96))*np.sin(Ktheta1)**(2.7-0.01*Kalpha) 
KY1SCF_B_MIP=1+0.65*Kbeta1*Ktau1**0.4*Kgamma**(1.09-0.77*Kbeta1)*np.sin(Ktheta1)**(0.06*Kgamma-1.16) 
KY1SCF_B_MOP=Ktau1**(-0.54)*Kgamma**(-0.05)*(0.99-0.47*Kbeta1+0.08*Kbeta1**4)*KY1SCF_C_MOP

KY2SCF_B_MOP=Ktau2**(-0.54)*Kgamma**(-0.05)*(0.99-0.47*Kbeta2+0.08*Kbeta2**4)*KY2SCF_C_MOP 
 
Kthetamax=np.maximum(Ktheta1,Ktheta2)

# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP_fatpack(sig,WT): 
    rang=fatpack.find_rainflow_ranges(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(1/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP(sig,WT): 
    rang, mean = rainflow_astm(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    sigt=np.maximum(mean+rang/2,0) 
    abssigc=np.maximum(rang/2-mean,0) 
    rang=rang*(sigt+0.8*abssigc)/(sigt+abssigc) 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    #N_failure=10**(12.03-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(0.5/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater without cathodic protection 
def damage_sea_noCP(sig,WT): 
    rang, mean = rainflow_astm(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    sigt=np.maximum(mean+rang/2,0) 
    abssigc=np.maximum(rang/2-mean,0) 
    rang=rang*(sigt+0.8*abssigc)/(sigt+abssigc) 
    N_failure=10**(12.03-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(0.5/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP_range_only(sig,WT): 
    rang, mean = rainflow_astm(sig) 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    #WT=16 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(rang*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(0.5/N_failure) 
    return Damage

def damage_seaCP_save3(sig,WT):

# Function to compute the damage for a stress history, for a tubular joint in seawater with cathodic protection 
def damage_seaCP_save(sig,WT): 
    cycles, ampl_edges, mean_edges = cycle_matrix2(sig, 50, 1) 
    cycles=cycles.reshape(50,) 
    #Central value of bins 
    bin_ampl=(ampl_edges[0:50]+ampl_edges[1:])/2 
    #Number of cycles to failure for the bin stress range 
    N_failure=np.maximum(10**(12.18-3*np.log10(bin_ampl*(WT/16)**0.25)),10**(16.13-5*np.log10(bin_ampl*(WT/16)**0.25))) 
    #Damage 
    Damage=np.sum(cycles/N_failure) 
    return Damage 
 
# Definition of a function to compute the 1Hz damage equivalent load of the selected 600s channel for the specified m 
exponent and number of bins 
def DEL_sig(sig,channelnum,mcurve,no_bins): 
# calculate the equivalent load manually for a random signal 
    eql = eq_load(sig[:,channelnum-1], no_bins=no_bins, m=mcurve, neq=600) 
    return eql[0][0] 
 
 
 
# Definition of a function to compute the 1Hz damage equivalent load of the selected 600s channel for the specified m 
exponent and number of bins 
def DEL(series,mcurve,no_bins): 
# calculate the equivalent load manually for a random signal 
    eql = eq_load(series, no_bins=no_bins, m=mcurve, neq=600) 
    return eql[0][0] 
 
 
 
# Definition of a function to compute the mean value of the selected channel 
def meanvalue(sig,channelnum): 
    return np.mean(sig[:,channelnum-1]) 
 
 
# Function to return the brace loads for a joint with 1 brace 
def Ybrace_loads(sig,Yclass): 
    Fz=sig[:,Yclass[5]+1] 
    Mx=sig[:,Yclass[5]+2] 
    My=sig[:,Yclass[5]+3] 
# Brace A Properties 
    node1_A = np.array([Yclass[13] ,  Yclass[14] ,  Yclass[15]]) 
    node2_A = np.array([Yclass[17] ,  Yclass[18] ,  Yclass[19]]) 
    eulpar_A = np.array([Yclass[6],   Yclass[7] ,  Yclass[8] , Yclass[9]]) 
    LENGTH_A =  np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
 
# Chord Properties 
    NODE1 = np.array([Yclass[48] ,  Yclass[49] ,  Yclass[50]]) 
    NODE2 = np.array([Yclass[52] ,  Yclass[53] ,  Yclass[54]]) 
    LENGTH =  np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
     
    Bladed2Hawc2 = np.array([[0, 1, 0], [1, 0, 0], [0, 0, -1]]) 
    node1_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node1_A.conj().T 
    node2_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node2_A.conj().T 
    NODE1 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE1.conj().T 
    NODE2 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE2.conj().T 
 
# Member directions 
    Vect_A = (node2_A - node1_A)/np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
    Vect_chord = (NODE2 - NODE1)/np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
 
# Side normal 
    Side_direct = 1/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord))*np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord) 
 
# Global to Local transformation matrix 
 
    Glob2Loca_A=np.zeros((3,3)) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][0] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 + (eulpar_A[1])**2 - (eulpar_A[2])**2 - (eulpar_A[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][1] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[2]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][2] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[3]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][0] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[2]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][1] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 - (eulpar_A[1])**2 + (eulpar_A[2])**2 - (eulpar_A[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][2] = 2*((eulpar_A[2]) * (eulpar_A[3]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[1])) 
==PAGE:134==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
133 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][0] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[3]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][1] = 2*((eulpar_A[2]) * (eulpar_A[3]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][2] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 - (eulpar_A[1])**2 - (eulpar_A[2])**2 + (eulpar_A[3])**2 
 
# Global to Structural transformation matrix for brace A 
    axis3 = -Vect_A                                          
    axis1 = Side_direct                                                  
    axis2 = np.cross(axis1, axis3)/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(axis1, axis3))                   
    Glob2Struc_A = np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).conj() 
#Glob2Struc_A = (np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).T).conj().T 
 
    Loca2Struc_A=Glob2Struc_A@np.linalg.pinv(Glob2Loca_A) 
     
    Fa=Loca2Struc_A[2][2]*Fz 
    Mip=Loca2Struc_A[0][0]*Mx+Loca2Struc_A[0][1]*My 
    Mop=Loca2Struc_A[1][0]*Mx+Loca2Struc_A[1][1]*My 
     
    return Fa, Mip, Mop 
 
 
 
 
 
 
# Function to return the brace loads for a joint with two braces 
def XKbrace_loads(sig,XKclass): 
    FzA=sig[:,XKclass[5]+1] 
    MxA=sig[:,XKclass[5]+2] 
    MyA=sig[:,XKclass[5]+3] 
     
    FzB=sig[:,XKclass[25]+1] 
    MxB=sig[:,XKclass[25]+2] 
    MyB=sig[:,XKclass[25]+3] 
# Brace A Properties 
    node1_A = np.array([XKclass[13] ,  XKclass[14] ,  XKclass[15]]) 
    node2_A = np.array([XKclass[17] ,  XKclass[18] ,  XKclass[19]]) 
    eulpar_A = np.array([XKclass[6],   XKclass[7] ,  XKclass[8] , XKclass[9]]) 
    LENGTH_A =  np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
     
# Brace B Properties 
    node1_B = np.array([XKclass[33] ,  XKclass[34] ,  XKclass[35]]) 
    node2_B = np.array([XKclass[37] ,  XKclass[38] ,  XKclass[39]]) 
    eulpar_B = np.array([XKclass[26],   XKclass[27] ,  XKclass[28] , XKclass[29]]) 
    LENGTH_B =  np.linalg.norm(node2_B - node1_B) 
 
# Chord Properties 
    NODE1 = np.array([XKclass[48] ,  XKclass[49] ,  XKclass[50]]) 
    NODE2 = np.array([XKclass[52] ,  XKclass[53] ,  XKclass[54]]) 
    LENGTH =  np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
     
    Bladed2Hawc2 = np.array([[0, 1, 0], [1, 0, 0], [0, 0, -1]]) 
    node1_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node1_A.conj().T 
    node2_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node2_A.conj().T 
    node1_B = Bladed2Hawc2@node1_B.conj().T 
    node2_B = Bladed2Hawc2@node2_B.conj().T 
    NODE1 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE1.conj().T 
    NODE2 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE2.conj().T 
 
# Member directions 
    Vect_A = (node2_A - node1_A)/np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
    Vect_B = (node2_B - node1_B)/np.linalg.norm(node2_B - node1_B) 
    Vect_chord = (NODE2 - NODE1)/np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
 
# Side normal 
    Side_directA = 1/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord))*np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord) 
    Side_directB = 1/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(Vect_B, Vect_chord))*np.cross(Vect_B, Vect_chord) 
 
# Global to Local transformation matrix 
 
    Glob2Loca_A=np.zeros((3,3)) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][0] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 + (eulpar_A[1])**2 - (eulpar_A[2])**2 - (eulpar_A[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][1] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[2]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][2] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[3]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][0] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[2]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][1] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 - (eulpar_A[1])**2 + (eulpar_A[2])**2 - (eulpar_A[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][2] = 2*((eulpar_A[2]) * (eulpar_A[3]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][0] = 2*((eulpar_A[1]) * (eulpar_A[3]) + (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][1] = 2*((eulpar_A[2]) * (eulpar_A[3]) - (eulpar_A[0]) * (eulpar_A[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][2] = (eulpar_A[0])**2 - (eulpar_A[1])**2 - (eulpar_A[2])**2 + (eulpar_A[3])**2 
     
    Glob2Loca_B=np.zeros((3,3)) 
    Glob2Loca_B[0][0] = (eulpar_B[0])**2 + (eulpar_B[1])**2 - (eulpar_B[2])**2 - (eulpar_B[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_B[0][1] = 2*((eulpar_B[1]) * (eulpar_B[2]) + (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[0][2] = 2*((eulpar_B[1]) * (eulpar_B[3]) - (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[1][0] = 2*((eulpar_B[1]) * (eulpar_B[2]) - (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[3])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[1][1] = (eulpar_B[0])**2 - (eulpar_B[1])**2 + (eulpar_B[2])**2 - (eulpar_B[3])**2 
    Glob2Loca_B[1][2] = 2*((eulpar_B[2]) * (eulpar_B[3]) + (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[2][0] = 2*((eulpar_B[1]) * (eulpar_B[3]) + (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[2])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[2][1] = 2*((eulpar_B[2]) * (eulpar_B[3]) - (eulpar_B[0]) * (eulpar_B[1])) 
    Glob2Loca_B[2][2] = (eulpar_B[0])**2 - (eulpar_B[1])**2 - (eulpar_B[2])**2 + (eulpar_B[3])**2 
 
# Global to Structural transformation matrix 
    axis3A = -Vect_A                                          
    axis1A = Side_directA                                                  
    axis2A = np.cross(axis1A, axis3A)/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(axis1A, axis3A))                   
    Glob2Struc_A = np.array([axis1A,axis2A,axis3A]).conj() 
==PAGE:135==
Online Master of Wind Energy – Final project report 
Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations 
134 
     
    axis3B = -Vect_B                                          
    axis1B = Side_directB                                                  
    axis2B = np.cross(axis1B, axis3B)/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(axis1B, axis3B))                   
    Glob2Struc_B = np.array([axis1B,axis2B,axis3B]).conj() 
     
#Glob2Struc_A = (np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).T).conj().T 
 
    Loca2Struc_A=Glob2Struc_A@np.linalg.pinv(Glob2Loca_A) 
    Loca2Struc_B=Glob2Struc_B@np.linalg.pinv(Glob2Loca_B) 
     
    FaA=Loca2Struc_A[2][2]*FzA 
    MipA=Loca2Struc_A[0][0]*MxA+Loca2Struc_A[0][1]*MyA 
    MopA=Loca2Struc_A[1][0]*MxA+Loca2Struc_A[1][1]*MyA 
     
    FaB=Loca2Struc_B[2][2]*FzB 
    MipB=Loca2Struc_B[0][0]*MxB+Loca2Struc_B[0][1]*MyB 
    MopB=Loca2Struc_B[1][0]*MxB+Loca2Struc_B[1][1]*MyB 
     
    return FaA, MipA, MopA, FaB, MipB, MopB 
 
 
 
 
 
# Function to return the node 10 brace 34 loads 
#def brace_loads(joint,brace,sig): 
def brace34_loads(sig): 
    Fz=sig[:,561] 
    Mx=sig[:,562] 
    My=sig[:,563] 
# Brace A Properties 
    node1_A = np.array([11.326500000000 ,  0.000000000000 ,  -39.354600000000]) 
    node2_A = np.array([10.653000000000 ,  -10.653000000000 ,  -30.209200000000]) 
    eulpar_A = np.array([-0.27974,   0.63527 ,  0.64942 , -0.31053]) 
    LENGTH_A =  np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
 
# Leg Properties 
    NODE1 = np.array([12.000000000000 ,  -12.000000000000 ,  -48.500000000000]) 
    NODE2 = np.array([9.631990000000 ,  -9.631990000000 ,  -16.344900000000]) 
    LENGTH =  np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
     
    Bladed2Hawc2 = np.array([[0, 1, 0], [1, 0, 0], [0, 0, -1]]) 
    node1_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node1_A.conj().T 
    node2_A = Bladed2Hawc2@node2_A.conj().T 
    NODE1 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE1.conj().T 
    NODE2 = Bladed2Hawc2@NODE2.conj().T 
 
# Member directions 
    Vect_A = (node2_A - node1_A)/np.linalg.norm(node2_A - node1_A) 
    Vect_chord = (NODE2 - NODE1)/np.linalg.norm(NODE2 - NODE1) 
        
#Connection angle 
    theta=min(np.arccos(np.vdot(Vect_A,Vect_chord)),np.pi-np.arccos(np.vdot(Vect_A,Vect_chord))) 
 
# Side normal 
    Side_direct = 1/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord))*np.cross(Vect_A, Vect_chord) 
 
# Global to Local transformation matrix 
    e0 = eulpar_A[0] 
    e1 = eulpar_A[1] 
    e2 = eulpar_A[2] 
    e3 = eulpar_A[3] 
 
    Glob2Loca_A=np.zeros((3,3)) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][0] = e0**2 + e1**2 - e2**2 - e3**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][1] = 2*(e1 * e2 + e0 * e3) 
    Glob2Loca_A[0][2] = 2*(e1 * e3 - e0 * e2) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][0] = 2*(e1 * e2 - e0 * e3) 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][1] = e0**2 - e1**2 + e2**2 - e3**2 
    Glob2Loca_A[1][2] = 2*(e2 * e3 + e0 * e1) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][0] = 2*(e1 * e3 + e0 * e2) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][1] = 2*(e2 * e3 - e0 * e1) 
    Glob2Loca_A[2][2] = e0**2 - e1**2 - e2**2 + e3**2 
 
# Global to Structural transformation matrix for brace A 
    axis3 = -Vect_A                                          
    axis1 = Side_direct                                                  
    axis2 = np.cross(axis1, axis3)/np.linalg.norm(np.cross(axis1, axis3))                   
    Glob2Struc_A = np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).conj() 
#Glob2Struc_A = (np.array([axis1,axis2,axis3]).T).conj().T 
 
    Loca2Struc_A=Glob2Struc_A@np.linalg.pinv(Glob2Loca_A) 
     
    Fa=Loca2Struc_A[2][2]*Fz 
    Mip=Loca2Struc_A[0][0]*Mx+Loca2Struc_A[0][1]*My 
    Mop=Loca2Struc_A[1][0]*Mx+Loca2Struc_A[1][1]*My 
     
    return Fa, Mip, Mop, theta

KY1SCF_C_AS=Kgamma*Ktau1**1.1*(1.11-3*(Kbeta1-0.52)**2)*np.sin(Ktheta1)**1.6+C1*(0.8*Kalpha-6)*Ktau1*Kbeta1**2*(1-
Kbeta1**2)**0.5*np.sin(2*Ktheta1)**2 
KY1SCF_C_MIP=1.45*Kbeta1*Ktau1**0.85*Kgamma**(1-0.68*Kbeta1)*np.sin(Ktheta1)**0.7 
KY1SCF_C_MOP=(Kgamma*Ktau1*Kbeta1*(1.7-1.05*Kbeta1**3)*np.sin(Ktheta1)**1.6)*(1-0.08*(Kbeta2*Kgamma)**0.5*np.exp(1)**(-
0.8*(1+Kzeta*np.sin(Ktheta1)/Kbeta1))) 
KY1SCF_B_AC=3+Kgamma**1.2*(0.12*np.exp(-4*Kbeta1.astype(float))+0.011*Kbeta1**2-0.045)+Kbeta1*Ktau1*(C3*Kalpha-1.2) 
KY1SCF_B_AS=1.3+Kgamma*Ktau1**0.52*Kalpha**0.1*(0.187-1.25*Kbeta1**1.1*(Kbeta1-0.96))*np.sin(Ktheta1)**(2.7-0.01*Kalpha) 
KY1SCF_B_MIP=1+0.65*Kbeta1*Ktau1**0.4*Kgamma**(1.09-0.77*Kbeta1)*np.sin(Ktheta1)**(0.06*Kgamma-1.16) 
KY1SCF_B_MOP=Ktau1**(-0.54)*Kgamma**(-0.05)*(0.99-0.47*Kbeta1+0.08*Kbeta1**4)*KY1SCF_C_MOP 
 
KY2SCF_C_AC=Kgamma**0.2*Ktau2*(2.65+5*(Kbeta2-0.65)**2)+Ktau2*Kbeta2*(C2*Kalpha-3)*np.sin(Ktheta2) 
KY2SCF_C_AS=Kgamma*Ktau2**1.1*(1.11-3*(Kbeta2-0.52)**2)*np.sin(Ktheta2)**1.6+C1*(0.8*Kalpha-6)*Ktau2*Kbeta2**2*(1-
Kbeta2**2)**0.5*np.sin(2*Ktheta2)**2 
KY2SCF_C_MIP=1.45*Kbeta2*Ktau2**0.85*Kgamma**(1-0.68*Kbeta2)*np.sin(Ktheta2)**0.7 
KY2SCF_C_MOP=(Kgamma*Ktau2*Kbeta2*(1.7-1.05*Kbeta2**3)*np.sin(Ktheta2)**1.6)*(1-0.08*(Kbeta1*Kgamma)**0.5*np.exp(1)**(-
0.8*(1+Kzeta*np.sin(Ktheta2)/Kbeta2)))

KY2SCF_B_AS=1.3+Kgamma*Ktau2**0.52*Kalpha**0.1*(0.187-1.25*Kbeta2**1.1*(Kbeta2-0.96))*np.sin(Ktheta2)**(2.7-0.01*Kalpha) 
KY2SCF_B_MIP=1+0.65*Kbeta2*Ktau2**0.4*Kgamma**(1.09-0.77*Kbeta2)*np.sin(Ktheta2)**(0.06*Kgamma-1.16) 
KY2SCF_B_MOP=Ktau2**(-0.54)*Kgamma**(-0.05)*(0.99-0.47*Kbeta2+0.08*Kbeta2**4)*KY2SCF_C_MOP 
 
Kthetamax=np.maximum(Ktheta1,Ktheta2) 
Kthetamin=np.minimum(Ktheta1,Ktheta2) 
Kbetamax=np.maximum(Kbeta1,Kbeta2) 
Kbetamin=np.minimum(Kbeta1,Kbeta2) 
 
KK1SCF_C_AC=Ktau1**0.9*Kgamma**0.5*(0.67-
Kbeta1**2+1.16*Kbeta1)*np.sin(Ktheta1)*(np.sin(Kthetamax)/np.sin(Kthetamin))**0.3*(Kbetamax/Kbetamin)**0.3*(1.64+0.29*Kbeta1

    BraceMaxDamage_K_B=Kclass[np.argmax(MaxDamage_K_B2)+1,24] 
MinJointLife_K_B=np.round(1/(3*OverallMaxDamage_K_B),2)

Attachment 24 – Matlab code for the verification of the damage computation 
clear all; 
 
% Brace A Properties 
od_A = 0.540000000000; 
wt_A = 0.020000000000; 
a_A = pi*wt_A*(od_A-wt_A) ; 
s_A = pi*(od_A^4-(od_A-2*wt_A)^4)/(32*od_A) ; 
node1_A = [12.0 12.0 -48.5]; 
node2_A = [0.0   11.3265   -39.3546]; 
eulpar_A = [0.28987 
0.62193 0.64496 -0.33646]; 
LENGTH_A =  pdist([node1_A;node2_A],'euclidean'); 
 
% Brace B Properties 
od_B =  0.54; 
wt_B = 0.02; 
a_B = pi*wt_B*(od_B-wt_B) ; 
s_B = pi*(od_B^4-(od_B-2*wt_B)^4)/(32*od_B) ; 
node1_B = [0.0   11.3265   -39.3546]; 
node2_B = [-10.653 10.653 -30.2092]; 
eulpar_B = [0.27097 
0.63146 0.65313 -0.31821]; 
LENGTH_B =  pdist([node1_B;node2_B],'euclidean'); 
 
% Leg Properties 
OD = 0.54; 
WT = 0.02; 
NODE1 = [10.653   10.653   -30.2092]; 
NODE2 = [-12.0   12.0   -48.5]; 
LENGTH =  pdist([NODE1;NODE2],'euclidean'); 
 
Bladed2Hawc2 = [0 1 0; 1 0 0; 0 0 -1]; 
node1_A = Bladed2Hawc2*node1_A'; node2_A = Bladed2Hawc2*node2_A'; 
node1_B = Bladed2Hawc2*node1_B'; node2_B = Bladed2Hawc2*node2_B'; 
NODE1 = Bladed2Hawc2*NODE1'; NODE2 = Bladed2Hawc2*NODE2'; 
 
% Member directions 
Vect_A = (node2_A - node1_A)./norm(node2_A - node1_A); 
Vect_B = (node2_B - node1_B)./norm(node2_B - node1_B); 
 
% Side normal 
Side_direct = cross(Vect_A, Vect_B)./norm(cross(Vect_A, Vect_B)); 
 
% Global to Local transformation matrix 
eulpar_X = eulpar_A; 
e0 = eulpar_X(1); e1 = eulpar_X(2); e2 = eulpar_X(3); e3 = eulpar_X(4); 
T(1,1) = e0^2 + e1^2 - e2^2 - e3^2; T(1,2) = 2*(e1 * e2 + e0 * e3); T(1,3) = 2*(e1 * e3 - e0 * e2); 
T(2,1) = 2*(e1 * e2 - e0 * e3); T(2,2) = e0^2 - e1^2 + e2^2 - e3^2; T(2,3) = 2*(e2 * e3 + e0 * e1); 
T(3,1) = 2*(e1 * e3 + e0 * e2); T(3,2) = 2*(e2 * e3 - e0 * e1); T(3,3) = e0^2 - e1^2 - e2^2 + e3^2; 
Glob2Loca_A = T; 
 
% Global to Structural transformation matrix for brace A 
axis3 = (-1)*Vect_A;                                               % Longitudinal axis 
axis1 = Side_direct;                                                        % Saddle-saddle axis 
axis2 = cross(axis1, axis3)./norm(cross(axis1, axis3));                     % Crown-crown axis 
Glob2Struc_A = [axis1,axis2,axis3]'; 
 
% Local to Structural transformation matrix for Brace A 
Loca2Struc_A = Glob2Struc_A/Glob2Loca_A; 
 
% Global to Local transformation matrix for brace B 
eulpar_X = eulpar_B;%EULPAR; 
e0 = eulpar_X(1); e1 = eulpar_X(2); e2 = eulpar_X(3); e3 = eulpar_X(4); 
T(1,1) = e0^2 + e1^2 - e2^2 - e3^2; T(1,2) = 2*(e1 * e2 + e0 * e3); T(1,3) = 2*(e1 * e3 - e0 * e2); 
T(2,1) = 2*(e1 * e2 - e0 * e3); T(2,2) = e0^2 - e1^2 + e2^2 - e3^2; T(2,3) = 2*(e2 * e3 + e0 * e1); 
T(3,1) = 2*(e1 * e3 + e0 * e2); T(3,2) = 2*(e2 * e3 - e0 * e1); T(3,3) = e0^2 - e1^2 - e2^2 + e3^2; 
Glob2Loca_B = T; 
 
% Global to Structural transformation matrix for brace B 
axis3 = -Vect_B;                                                             % Longitudinal axis 
axis1 = Side_direct;                                                        % Saddle-saddle axis 
axis2 = cross(axis1, axis3)./norm(cross(axis1, axis3));                     % Crown-crown axis 
Glob2Struc_B = [axis1,axis2,axis3]'; 
 
% Local to Structural transformation matrix for brace B 
Loca2Struc_B = Glob2Struc_B/Glob2Loca_B; 
 
SimTime = 600;                                      % Simulation time in second 
SimStep = 0.02;                                     % Simulation time step in second 
TimePointNber = SimTime/SimStep; 
% Load sensors for analyzing 
Fz_matA = 625; Mx_matA = Fz_matA+1; My_matA = Fz_matA+2; 
Fz_matB = 595; Mx_matB = Fz_matB+1; My_matB = Fz_matB+2; 
 
[A_file,FreqSim,TimeSim,Binary]  = ReadHawc2('C:\HAWC2\10MW_Jacket_IEC_64\send\dlc12_ntm_wsp19_s9007_wd180'); 
Time=A_file(:,1);     
    for j=1:TimePointNber 
         
        % Directional Loads in local coord system 
        FzA = 1000 * A_file(j,Fz_matA);                  % Axial force [N] 
        MxA = 1000 * A_file(j,Mx_matA);                  % x-direct moment [Nm] 
        MyA = 1000 * A_file(j,My_matA);                  % y-direct moment [Nm] 
        FzB = 1000 * A_file(j,Fz_matB);                  % Axial force [N] 
        MxB = 1000 * A_file(j,Mx_matB);                  % x-direct moment [Nm] 
        MyB = 1000 * A_file(j,My_matB);                  % y-direct moment [Nm]

Quantification of variability in jacket fatigue damage from environmental model variations

        % Directional Loads in Structural coord system of Brace A 
        NaA(j) = Loca2Struc_A(3,3)*FzA; %axial force 
        MipA(j) = Loca2Struc_A(1,1:2)*[MxA;MyA]; 
        MopA(j) = Loca2Struc_A(2,1:2)*[MxA;MyA]; 
         
        % Directional Loads in Structural coord system of Brace B 
        NaB(j) = Loca2Struc_B(3,3)*FzB; %axial force 
        MipB(j) = Loca2Struc_B(1,1:2)*[MxB;MyB]; 
        MopB(j) = Loca2Struc_B(2,1:2)*[MxB;MyB]; 
    end 
 
   
figure(1) 
hold on 
plot(NaA) 
plot(NaB) 
grid on 
xlabel('Time [s]') 
ylabel('Brace axial load [N]') 
title('Na')

title('Mip')

sigx_A = NaA/a_A ; 
sigmy_A= MipA/s_A ; 
sigmz_A= MopA/s_A ; 
sigx_B = NaB/a_B ; 
sigmy_B= MipB/s_B ; 
sigmz_B= MopB/s_B ; 
 
SCF_AS_B=5.12604390683746 ; 
SCF_MOP_B=2.917293079821302 ; 
 
sig_3_C_B=(SCF_AS_B*sigx_B-SCF_MOP_B*sigmz_B)/10^6; 
 
rain=rainflow(sig_3_C_B); 
nb=rain(:,1); 
meanv=rain(:,3); 
rang=rain(:,2); 
sigt=max(meanv+rang/2,0); 
abssigc=max(rang/2-meanv,0); 
ranges=rang.*(sigt+0.8*abssigc)./(sigt+abssigc); 
 
N_failure=max(10.^(12.18-3*log10(ranges*(wt_A/0.016)^0.25)), 10.^(16.13-5*log10(ranges*(wt_A/0.016)^0.25))); 
 
Damage=sum(nb./N_failure)
